                                   class="mq640-hide inline-block pin-left inline-group-top pa10 pr15 pl15 small-header-icons background-bluegray-translucent text11 contrast-text mr1"

                                   data-track-dest="link:Submit">

                            <h1 class="tighten-line-height small-space-below" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Novel Type II and Monomeric NAD<sup>+</sup> Specific Isocitrate Dehydrogenases: Phylogenetic Affinity, Enzymatic Characterization, and Evolutionary Implication</h1>
                            <ul class="grid grid-12 last clean-list inline-list js-list-authors text14" data-etal=25 data-etal-small=3><li itemprop=author itemscope itemtype=http://schema.org/Person><span class=js-separator></span><a href=#auth-1><span itemprop=name>Peng Wang</span></a><sup class=js-hide><a href=#a1>1</a><span itemprop=affiliation itemscope itemtype=http://schema.org/Organization class=hide><meta itemprop=name content="Institute of Molecular Biology and Biotechnology, College of Life Sciences, Anhui Normal University"><meta itemprop=address content="Institute of Molecular Biology and Biotechnology, College of Life Sciences, Anhui Normal University, No.1 Beijing East Road, Wuhu 241000, Anhui, China"></span></sup><li itemprop=author itemscope itemtype=http://schema.org/Person><span class=js-separator>, </span><a href=#auth-2><span itemprop=name>Changqi Lv</span></a><sup class=js-hide><a href=#a1>1</a><span itemprop=affiliation itemscope itemtype=http://schema.org/Organization class=hide><meta itemprop=name content="Institute of Molecular Biology and Biotechnology, College of Life Sciences, Anhui Normal University"><meta itemprop=address content="Institute of Molecular Biology and Biotechnology, College of Life Sciences, Anhui Normal University, No.1 Beijing East Road, Wuhu 241000, Anhui, China"></span></sup><li itemprop=author itemscope itemtype=http://schema.org/Person><span class=js-separator>&nbsp;&amp; </span><a href=#auth-3 class="icon icon-right-top icon-mail-12x9-blue pr15" data-corresp-id=c3><span itemprop=name>Guoping Zhu</span></a><sup class=js-hide><a href=#a1>1</a><span itemprop=affiliation itemscope itemtype=http://schema.org/Organization class=hide><meta itemprop=name content="Institute of Molecular Biology and Biotechnology, College of Life Sciences, Anhui Normal University"><meta itemprop=address content="Institute of Molecular Biology and Biotechnology, College of Life Sciences, Anhui Normal University, No.1 Beijing East Road, Wuhu 241000, Anhui, China"></span></sup></ul>
                            
                            
                            <div class="flex-box grid-12 mq480-kill-flex standard-space-below cleared" data-container-section=info><div class="flex-box grid grid-6 mq480-grid-12"><ul class="flex-box-item none border-gray-medium border-left-1 text14 ma0 pa0 pl10" data-component=article-info-list><li><i>Scientific Reports</i> <b>5</b>, Article&nbsp;number:&nbsp;9150 (2015)<li><abbr title="Digital Object Identifier">doi</abbr>:10.1038/srep09150<li><a href=/articles/srep09150.ris data-track=click data-track-source=citation-download data-track-dest="link:Download Citation">Download Citation</a></ul></div><div class="flex-box grid grid-6 last mq480-grid-12"><div class="flex-box-item border-gray-medium border-left-1 text14 pl10 mq480-pt10" data-component=article-history-list><dl><dt class="pin-left clear-left text-gray-light pr6">Received:<dd><time datetime=2014-10-08>08 October 2014</time><dt class="pin-left clear-left text-gray-light pr6">Accepted:<dd><time datetime=2015-02-23>23 February 2015</time><dt class="pin-left clear-left text-gray-light pr6">Published online:<dd><time datetime=2015-03-16 itemprop=datePublished>16 March 2015</time></dl></div></div></div>
                            
                        </div>
                    </header>
                    
    <ul class="mb0 pa0 tiny-space-above inline-list text14 js-hide" data-component="article-subject-links">
        <li><a href="/subjects/enzymes" class="subject-tag-link mr2 mb2" data-track="click" data-track-source="subject-name" data-track-dest="link:Enzymes">Enzymes</a></li><li><a href="/subjects/phylogenetics" class="subject-tag-link mr2 mb2" data-track="click" data-track-source="subject-name" data-track-dest="link:Phylogenetics">Phylogenetics</a></li>
    </ul>

                    
    <ul class="clean-list mb0 tiny-space-above js-hide" data-component="article-relations">
        
    </ul>


                    <div data-article-body="true" class="article-body clear">
                        <section aria-labelledby=abstract><div class="serif article-section js-article-section cleared clear" id=abstract-section><h2 class="js-section-title section-title position-relative strong tighten-line-height background-gray-light pt20 pb6 pl20 pr20 standard-space-below mq640-pt20 mq640-pb10 mq640-mt0 js-extend-left" id=abstract>Abstract</h2><div class="pl20 mq875-pl0 js-collapsible-section" id=abstract-content itemprop=description><p>NAD<sup>+</sup> use is an ancestral trait of isocitrate dehydrogenase (IDH), and the NADP<sup>+</sup> phenotype arose through evolution as an ancient adaptation event. However, no NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific IDHs have been found among type II IDHs and monomeric IDHs. In this study, novel type II homodimeric NAD-IDHs from <i>Ostreococcus lucimarinus</i> CCE9901 IDH (OlIDH) and <i>Micromonas</i> sp. RCC299 (MiIDH), and novel monomeric NAD-IDHs from <i>Campylobacter</i> sp. FOBRC14 IDH (CaIDH) and <i>Campylobacter curvus</i> (CcIDH) were reported for the first time. The homodimeric OlIDH and monomeric CaIDH were determined by size exclusion chromatography and MALDI-TOF/TOF mass spectrometry. All the four IDHs were demonstrated to be NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific, since OlIDH, MiIDH, CaIDH and CcIDH displayed 99-fold, 224-fold, 61-fold and 37-fold preferences for NAD<sup>+</sup> over NADP<sup>+</sup>, respectively. The putative coenzyme discriminating amino acids (Asp326/Met327 in OlIDH, Leu584/Asp595 in CaIDH) were evaluated, and the coenzyme specificities of the two mutants, OlIDH R<sup>326</sup>H<sup>327</sup> and CaIDH H<sup>584</sup>R<sup>595</sup>, were completely reversed from NAD<sup>+</sup> to NADP<sup>+</sup>. The detailed biochemical properties, including optimal reaction pH and temperature, thermostability, and metal ion effects, of OlIDH and CaIDH were further investigated. The evolutionary connections among OlIDH, CaIDH, and all the other forms of IDHs were described and discussed thoroughly.</p></div></div></section>

                        
                            <section aria-labelledby=introduction><div class="serif article-section js-article-section cleared clear" id=introduction-section><h2 class="js-section-title section-title position-relative strong tighten-line-height background-gray-light pt20 pb6 pl20 pr20 standard-space-below mq640-pt20 mq640-pb10 mq640-mt0 js-extend-left small-space-above" id=introduction>Introduction</h2><div class="pl20 mq875-pl0 js-collapsible-section" id=introduction-content><p>The progressive sequencing of complete biological genomes has dramatically increased the size of protein databases. The expansion of protein information, most of which are functionally annotated by computational techniques, will consequently increase the diversity of each protein family, thus providing us with an opportunity to extend and refine the classification of protein families. Protein phylogenetic analysis has always been important, because it can provide insight into protein evolution and further implicate protein function. In the present study, we applied this principle to explore novel isocitrate dehydrogenases (IDHs).</p><p>IDH is a key enzyme in the tricarboxylic acid (TCA) cycle. It catalyzes the oxidative decarboxylation of isocitrate to α-ketoglutarate (α-KG) and CO<sub>2</sub>, which is accompanied by the reduction of NAD(P<sup>+</sup>) to NAD(P)H. The IDH reaction provides organisms with not only energy but also biosynthetic precursors, such as α-KG, for metabolism. Thus, these metabolic pathways are among the first to have evolved<sup><a id=ref-link-section-1 title="Amend, J. P. &amp; Shock, E. L. Energetics of amino acid synthesis in hydrothermal ecosystems. Science 281, 1659–1662 (1998)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref1>1</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-2 title="Melendez-Hevia, E., Waddell, T. G. &amp; Cascante, M. The puzzle of the Krebs citric acid cycle: assembling the pieces of chemically feasible reactions, and opportunism in the design of metabolic pathways during evolution. J. Mol. Evol. 43, 293–303 (1996)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref2>2</a></sup>. Consequently, IDHs are ubiquitously distributed throughout the three domains of life: Archaea, Bacteria, and Eukarya. Based on coenzyme specificity, the IDH family can be divided into NAD<sup>+</sup>-dependent IDHs (EC 1.1.1.41, NAD-IDHs) and NADP<sup>+</sup>-dependent IDHs (EC 1.1.1.41, NADP-IDHs). IDHs with different coenzyme dependencies play varying roles <i>in vivo</i>. NAD-IDH catalysis generates NADH, which participates in energy metabolism. NADP-IDH catalysis generates NADPH, which is an important source of reducing power. NADPH also plays a role in the cellular defense against oxidative damage and the detoxification of reactive oxygen species<sup><a id=ref-link-section-3 title="Taylor, A. B., Hu, G., Hart, P. J. &amp; McAlister-Henn, L. Allosteric motions in structures of yeast NAD+-specific isocitrate dehydrogenase. J. Biol. Chem. 283, 10872–10880, 10.1074/jbc.M708719200 (2008)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref3>3</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-4 title="Zhu, G., Golding, G. B. &amp; Dean, A. M. The selective cause of an ancient adaptation. Science 307, 1279–1282, 10.1126/science.1106974 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref4>4</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-5 title="Jo, S. H. et al. Control of mitochondrial redox balance and cellular defense against oxidative damage by mitochondrial NADP+-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase. J. Biol. Chem. 276, 16168–16176, 10.1074/jbc.M010120200 (2001)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref5>5</a></sup>.</p><p>Most studies have focused on the general understanding of the biochemistry, structure, and evolution of IDH. Meanwhile, the potential applications of IDH in biotechnology and drug design against pathogens have also been recently investigated. An NAD-IDH with poor performance in decarboxylating from <i>Zymomonas mobilis</i> serves as a potential genetic modification target towards optimized <i>Z</i>. <i>mobilis</i> strains to produce ethanol<sup><a id=ref-link-section-6 title="Wang, P., Jin, M. &amp; Zhu, G. Biochemical and molecular characterization of NAD(+)-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase from the ethanologenic bacterium Zymomonas mobilis. FEMS Microbiol. Lett. 327, 134–141, 10.1111/j.1574-6968.2011.02467.x (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref6>6</a></sup>. The characterization of NADP-IDH from <i>Microcystis aeruginosa</i> may provide new ideas for controlling blue-green algae through biological techniques<sup><a id=ref-link-section-7 title="Jin, M. M. et al. Biochemical characterization of NADP(+)-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase from Microcystis aeruginosa PCC7806. Mol. Biol. Rep. 40, 2995–3002, 10.1007/s11033-012-2371-8 (2013)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref7>7</a></sup>. IDHs from pathogenic bacteria, such as <i>Plasmodium falciparum</i>, <i>Mycobacteriu tuberculosis</i>, and <i>Leptospira interrogans</i>, have been reported as drug targets, because they stand at a branch point of the TCA cycle and glyoxylate shunt<sup><a id=ref-link-section-8 title="Chan, M. &amp; Sim, T. S. Recombinant Plasmodium falciparum NADP-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase is active and harbours a unique 26 amino acid tail. Exp. Parasitol. 103, 120–126 (2003)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref8>8</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-9 title="Quartararo, C. E., Hazra, S., Hadi, T. &amp; Blanchard, J. S. Structural, kinetic and chemical mechanism of isocitrate dehydrogenase-1 from Mycobacterium tuberculosis. Biochemistry 52, 1765–1775, 10.1021/bi400037w (2013)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref9>9</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-10 title="Zhao, X., Wang, P., Zhu, G., Wang, B. &amp; Zhu, G. Enzymatic characterization of a type II isocitrate dehydrogenase from pathogenic Leptospira interrogans serovar Lai strain 56601. Appl. Biochem. Biotechnol. 172, 487–496, 10.1007/s12010-013-0521-7 (2014)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref10>10</a></sup>. IDHs are also ideal immuno-diagnostic candidates, due to their highly conserved housekeeping function. For example, <i>M. tuberculosis</i> IDHs elicit strong B-cell responses in tuberculosis (TB)-infected populations and can differentiate between healthy vaccinated and TB populations<sup><a id=ref-link-section-11 title="Banerjee, S. et al. Mycobacterium tuberculosis (Mtb) isocitrate dehydrogenases show strong B cell response and distinguish vaccinated controls from TB patients. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 101, 12652–12657, 10.1073/pnas.0404347101 (2004)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref11>11</a></sup>. In addition, <i>Helicobacter pylori</i> IDH can be an immunogen that interacts with the host immune system to subsequently lead to possible autolytic release and significantly elicit humoral responses in individuals with invasive <i>H. pylori</i> infection<sup><a id=ref-link-section-12 title="Hussain, M. A. et al. Isocitrate dehydrogenase of Helicobacter pylori potentially induces humoral immune response in subjects with peptic ulcer disease and gastritis. PLoS One 3, e1481, 10.1371/journal.pone.0001481 (2008)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref12>12</a></sup>.</p><p>Besides pathogenic bacterial IDH, human cytosolic NADP-IDH (IDH1) and mitochondrial NADP-IDH (IDH2) have been considered as drug targets. Mutations in IDH1 and IDH2 are frequently identified in various cancers, such as glioblastoma multiforme and acute myeloid leukemia<sup><a id=ref-link-section-13 title="Parsons, D. W. et al. An integrated genomic analysis of human glioblastoma multiforme. Science 321, 1807–1812, 10.1126/science.1164382 (2008)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref13>13</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-14 title="Mardis, E. R. et al. Recurring mutations found by sequencing an acute myeloid leukemia genome. N Engl J Med 361, 1058–1066, 10.1056/NEJMoa0903840 (2009)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref14>14</a></sup>. Heterozygous IDH mutations are remarkably specific to a single codon in the conserved and functionally important arginine 132 residue (R132) of IDH1 and 172 residue (R172) of IDH2. Mutations result in the simultaneous loss of normal IDH catalytic activity. However, the production of α-KG and NADPH grants mutated IDHs with the neomorphic activity of reducing α-KG to 2-hydroxyglutarate (2-HG), which is accompanied by the oxidation of NADPH to NADP<sup>+</sup> <sup><a id=ref-link-section-15 title="Dang, L. et al. Cancer-associated IDH1 mutations produce 2-hydroxyglutarate. Nature 462, 739–744, 10.1038/nature08617 (2009)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref15>15</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-16 title="Ward, P. S. et al. The common feature of leukemia-associated IDH1 and IDH2 mutations is a neomorphic enzyme activity converting alpha-ketoglutarate to 2-hydroxyglutarate. Cancer Cell 17, 225–234, 10.1016/j.ccr.2010.01.020 (2010)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref16>16</a></sup>. The accumulation of 2-HG competitively inhibits α-KG-dependent enzymes, thus causing cellular alterations in epigenetics, collagen maturation, and hypoxia signaling<sup><a id=ref-link-section-17 title="Lu, C. et al. IDH mutation impairs histone demethylation and results in a block to cell differentiation. Nature 483, 474–478, 10.1038/nature10860 (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref17>17</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-18 title="Turcan, S. et al. IDH1 mutation is sufficient to establish the glioma hypermethylator phenotype. Nature 483, 479–483, 10.1038/nature10866 (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref18>18</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-19 title="Reitman, Z. J. et al. Cancer-associated Isocitrate Dehydrogenase 1 (IDH1) R132H Mutation and d-2-Hydroxyglutarate Stimulate Glutamine Metabolism under Hypoxia. J. Biol. Chem. 289, 23318–23328, 10.1074/jbc.M114.575183 (2014)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref19>19</a></sup>.</p><p>As an ancient enzyme, IDH acquired various primary structures and different oligomeric states through evolution. Four kinds of IDHs have been reported: monomer, homo-dimer, homo-tetramer, and hetero-oligomer. Monomeric IDHs have been characterized from various eubacteria, and all of them are highly specific to NADP<sup>+</sup> <sup><a id=ref-link-section-20 title="Imabayashi, F., Aich, S., Prasad, L. &amp; Delbaere, L. T. Substrate-free structure of a monomeric NADP isocitrate dehydrogenase: an open conformation phylogenetic relationship of isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proteins 63, 100–112, 10.1002/prot.20867 (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref20>20</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-21 title="Yasutake, Y. et al. Crystal structure of the monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase in the presence of NADP+: insight into the cofactor recognition, catalysis, and evolution. J. Biol. Chem. 278, 36897–36904, 10.1074/jbc.M304091200 (2003)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref21>21</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-22 title="Zhang, B. et al. Enzymatic characterization of a monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from Streptomyces lividans TK54. Biochimie 91, 1405–1410, 10.1016/j.biochi.2009.07.011 (2009)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref22>22</a></sup>. Because the amino acid sequence identities are &lt;10% between monomeric IDHs and other types of IDHs, this group has been recognized as a separate clade that evolved independently<sup><a id=ref-link-section-23 title="Imabayashi, F., Aich, S., Prasad, L. &amp; Delbaere, L. T. Substrate-free structure of a monomeric NADP isocitrate dehydrogenase: an open conformation phylogenetic relationship of isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proteins 63, 100–112, 10.1002/prot.20867 (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref20>20</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-24 title="Dean, A. M. &amp; Golding, G. B. Protein engineering reveals ancient adaptive replacements in isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 94, 3104–3109 (1997)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref23>23</a></sup>. Dimeric and multimeric IDHs have been divided into three phylogenetic subfamilies<sup><a id=ref-link-section-25 title="Dean, A. M. &amp; Golding, G. B. Protein engineering reveals ancient adaptive replacements in isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 94, 3104–3109 (1997)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref23>23</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-26 title="Steen, I. H., Lien, T. &amp; Birkeland, N. K. Biochemical and phylogenetic characterization of isocitrate dehydrogenase from a hyperthermophilic archaeon, Archaeoglobus fulgidus. Arch. Microbiol. 168, 412–420 (1997)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref24>24</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-27 title="Steen, I. H. et al. Comparison of isocitrate dehydrogenase from three hyperthermophiles reveals differences in thermostability, cofactor specificity, oligomeric state, and phylogenetic affiliation. J. Biol. Chem. 276, 43924–43931, 10.1074/jbc.M105999200 (2001)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref25>25</a></sup>. Subfamily I is a prokaryotic group, in which NAD<sup>+</sup> and NADP<sup>+</sup> usage is widespread within archaeal and eubacterial homo-dimeric enzymes. Subfamily II is mainly composed of eukaryotic homo-dimeric NADP-IDHs, with a small number of eubacterial homo-dimeric NADP-IDHs. Subfamily III is comprised of mitochondrial hetero-oligomeric NAD-IDHs and eubacterial homo-tetrameric enzymes with either NAD<sup>+</sup> or NADP<sup>+</sup> as the cofactor<sup><a id=ref-link-section-28 title="Stokke, R. et al. Biochemical characterization of isocitrate dehydrogenase from Methylococcus capsulatus reveals a unique NAD+-dependent homotetrameric enzyme. Arch. Microbiol. 187, 361–370, 10.1007/s00203-006-0200-y (2007)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref26>26</a></sup>.</p><p>Because subfamily III IDHs share more than 30% sequence identities with subfamily I members but less than 15% sequence identities with subfamily II counterparts, subfamilies III and I can be combined accordingly. Therefore, we simply divided the IDH protein family into type I IDHs (subfamily I and III) and type II IDHs (subfamily II) in our previous study<sup><a id=ref-link-section-29 title="Zhu, G., Golding, G. B. &amp; Dean, A. M. The selective cause of an ancient adaptation. Science 307, 1279–1282, 10.1126/science.1106974 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref4>4</a></sup>. Three total types of IDH can be distinguished: type I IDH, type II IDH, and monomeric IDH. Interestingly, both NAD<sup>+</sup> and NADP<sup>+</sup> are broadly utilized among type I IDHs. However, homo-dimeric type II IDHs and monomeric IDHs are all NADP<sup>+</sup> specific<sup><a id=ref-link-section-30 title="Zhu, G., Golding, G. B. &amp; Dean, A. M. The selective cause of an ancient adaptation. Science 307, 1279–1282, 10.1126/science.1106974 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref4>4</a></sup>. We previously demonstrated that NAD<sup>+</sup> use is an ancestral trait of IDH, and the NADP<sup>+</sup> phenotype of eubacterial dimeric NADP-IDH arose on or about the time that eukaryotic mitochondria first appeared (about 3.5 billion years ago) to synthesize NADPH for the adaptation of bacterial growth on acetate<sup><a id=ref-link-section-31 title="Zhu, G., Golding, G. B. &amp; Dean, A. M. The selective cause of an ancient adaptation. Science 307, 1279–1282, 10.1126/science.1106974 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref4>4</a></sup>. Consequently, NADP<sup>+</sup>-specific type II IDHs and monomeric IDHs may also have their own NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific ancestors. However, no NAD-IDHs that belong to these two subfamilies have been explored.</p><p>The rapid growth of the IDH protein “pool”, which has been contributed by various genome projects, should allow us to search for putative NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific type II IDHs and monomeric IDHs. In the present study, four novel NAD-IDHs that belong to the families of type II IDHs and monomeric IDHs were reported for the first time. Enzymatic properties of these NAD-IDHs, including the oligomeric state in solution, optimum pH and temperature for catalysis, thermostability, and metal ion dependency, were characterized thoroughly. Kinetic parameters of the two IDHs towards coenzymes were determined in detail, and their coenzyme-binding sites were evaluated by site-directed mutagenesis. The discovery of these novel NAD-IDHs will refine the chemistry and phylogeny of IDH and provide new insights into the evolution of this ancient protein family.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby=results><div class="serif article-section js-article-section cleared clear" id=results-section><h2 class="js-section-title section-title position-relative strong tighten-line-height background-gray-light pt20 pb6 pl20 pr20 standard-space-below mq640-pt20 mq640-pb10 mq640-mt0 js-extend-left small-space-above" id=results>Results</h2><div class="pl20 mq875-pl0 js-collapsible-section" id=results-content><h2 class="h3 strong mb4">Phylogenetic Analysis and Sequence Alignment</h2><p>In our previous study, we divided the IDH protein family into type I IDHs and type II IDHs based on sequence homology<sup><a id=ref-link-section-32 title="Zhu, G., Golding, G. B. &amp; Dean, A. M. The selective cause of an ancient adaptation. Science 307, 1279–1282, 10.1126/science.1106974 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref4>4</a></sup>. Herein, we expanded the classification of this ancient family by incorporating new members (<a href=#f1>Fig. 1</a>). Monomeric IDHs were included, although their overall sequence homologies with other forms of IDH are relatively low (≈10%)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-33 title="Zhang, B. et al. Enzymatic characterization of a monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from Streptomyces lividans TK54. Biochimie 91, 1405–1410, 10.1016/j.biochi.2009.07.011 (2009)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref22>22</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-34 title="Chen, R. &amp; Yang, H. A highly specific monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from Corynebacterium glutamicum. Arch Biochem Biophys 383, 238–245, 10.1006/abbi.2000.2082 (2000)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref27>27</a></sup>. Consequently, they constituted a monophylogenic clade on the phylogenetic tree (<a href=#f1>Fig. 1</a>). Therefore, three main IDH subfamilies (type I IDH, type II IDH and monomeric IDH) were identified to comprise the whole IDH protein family. Members in IDH subfamily were further classified into eleven subgroups with regard to different coenzyme specificity, different oligomeric states and diverse resources (<a href=#f1>Fig. 1</a>). Different tree building algorithms were also employed, such as UPGMA method, Maximum Likelihood method and Minimum Evolution method. All trees contain three well-supported monophyletic groups: type I IDHs, type II IDHs, and monomeric IDHs (see <a href=/articles/srep09150#s1>Supplementary Fig. S3, Fig. S4 and Fig. S5</a> online). When an outgroup of malate dehydrogenase (MDH) was added into the phylogenetic study, the overall topology of the evolutionary tree remained very similar (see <a href=/articles/srep09150#s1>Supplementary Fig. S6</a> online).</p><div class="border-gray-medium border-all-5 standard-space-below pl10 pr10 pt20 pb20 clear" data-container-section=figure><figure id=Fig1><figcaption><b id=f1 class="block tiny-space-below">Figure 1: Evolutionary relationships of 197 IDHs from diverse background.</b></figcaption><div class=small-space-below><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/1 class="block small-space-below" data-track=click data-track-source=image data-track-dest=link:Figure1><img src=https://images.nature.com/m685/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-f1.jpg alt="Figure 1" class="block max-width" itemprop=image></a><div class="suppress-bottom-margin add-top-margin text14 sans-serif"><p>The evolutionary history was inferred using the Neighbor-Joining method. The percentage of replicate trees in which the associated taxa clustered together in the bootstrap test (500 replicates) are shown next to the branches. The tree is drawn to scale, with branch lengths in the same units as those of the evolutionary distances used to infer the phylogenetic tree. Phylogenetic analyses were conducted in MEGA6. The IDH sequences used were listed in <a href=/articles/srep09150#s1>Table S1</a>. OlIDH and CaIDH were marked by “<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m1.jpg alt="" class=inline>” and “<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m2.jpg alt="" class=inline>” respectively.</p></div></div><div class="text-right hide-print"><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/1 class=" ml10 sans-serif pill-button inline-block" data-track=click data-track-source=button data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image"><span>Full size image</span></a></div></figure></div><p>In the type I subgroup, the most common ones are the eubacterial homodimeric NADP- and NAD-IDHs, which are represented by <i>E. coli</i> IDH and <i>Z. mobilis</i> IDH, respectively<sup><a id=ref-link-section-35 title="Wang, P., Jin, M. &amp; Zhu, G. Biochemical and molecular characterization of NAD(+)-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase from the ethanologenic bacterium Zymomonas mobilis. FEMS Microbiol. Lett. 327, 134–141, 10.1111/j.1574-6968.2011.02467.x (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref6>6</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-36 title="Dean, A. M. &amp; Golding, G. B. Protein engineering reveals ancient adaptive replacements in isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 94, 3104–3109 (1997)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref23>23</a></sup>. The pairwise amino acid sequence identity among this subgroup was more than 50%. Mitochondrial hetero-oligomeric NAD-IDHs, together with a batch of eubacterial homotetrameric NAD-IDHs, were grouped into a single branch in the type I subfamily. More than 45% identity exists between these eukaryotic and prokaryotic NAD-IDHs<sup><a id=ref-link-section-37 title="Stokke, R. et al. Biochemical characterization of isocitrate dehydrogenase from Methylococcus capsulatus reveals a unique NAD+-dependent homotetrameric enzyme. Arch. Microbiol. 187, 361–370, 10.1007/s00203-006-0200-y (2007)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref26>26</a></sup>. Although this branch was designated as subfamily III in other IDH phylogenetic studies<sup><a id=ref-link-section-38 title="Imabayashi, F., Aich, S., Prasad, L. &amp; Delbaere, L. T. Substrate-free structure of a monomeric NADP isocitrate dehydrogenase: an open conformation phylogenetic relationship of isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proteins 63, 100–112, 10.1002/prot.20867 (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref20>20</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-39 title="Steen, I. H., Lien, T. &amp; Birkeland, N. K. Biochemical and phylogenetic characterization of isocitrate dehydrogenase from a hyperthermophilic archaeon, Archaeoglobus fulgidus. Arch. Microbiol. 168, 412–420 (1997)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref24>24</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-40 title="Steen, I. H. et al. Comparison of isocitrate dehydrogenase from three hyperthermophiles reveals differences in thermostability, cofactor specificity, oligomeric state, and phylogenetic affiliation. J. Biol. Chem. 276, 43924–43931, 10.1074/jbc.M105999200 (2001)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref25>25</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-41 title="Vinekar, R., Verma, C. &amp; Ghosh, I. Functional relevance of dynamic properties of Dimeric NADP-dependent Isocitrate Dehydrogenases. BMC Bioinformatics 13 Suppl 17, S2, 10.1186/1471-2105-13-S17-S2 (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref28>28</a></sup>, it is reasonable to encompass it into the type I subfamily, because proteins in this branch exhibit more than 30% identity with type I eubacterial homodimeric IDHs. A small group of IDHs, which were represented by <i>Rickettsia</i> IDH and <i>Thermus thermophilus</i> IDH, was branched before the mitochondrial group. These IDHs were structurally distinguished from other dimeric IDHs, as they were longer in the C-terminal region. Although they were assigned as subfamily IV IDHs in two recent studies<sup><a id=ref-link-section-42 title="Vinekar, R., Verma, C. &amp; Ghosh, I. Functional relevance of dynamic properties of Dimeric NADP-dependent Isocitrate Dehydrogenases. BMC Bioinformatics 13 Suppl 17, S2, 10.1186/1471-2105-13-S17-S2 (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref28>28</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-43 title="Kumar, S. M. et al. Crystal structure studies of NADP+ dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase from Thermus thermophilus exhibiting a novel terminal domain. Biochem. Biophys. Res. Commun. 449, 107–113, 10.1016/j.bbrc.2014.04.164 (2014)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref29>29</a></sup>, they were included as type I IDHs in our study, as they shared considerable sequence identities (&gt;40%) with mitochondrial NAD-IDHs.</p><p>The type II subfamily was comprised of homodimeric NADP-IDHs from eukaryotes and eubacteria (<a href=#f1>Fig. 1</a>). A small group of NADP-IDHs, which are represented by <i>Thermotoga maritima</i> IDH (TmIDH)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-44 title="Karlstrom, M. et al. The crystal structure of a hyperthermostable subfamily II isocitrate dehydrogenase from Thermotoga maritima. FEBS J. 273, 2851–2868, 10.1111/j.1742-4658.2006.05298.x (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref30>30</a></sup>, branched clearly before the well-characterized homodimeric NADP-IDH clade. Although the TmIDH-like proteins were highly identical (&gt;50%) to type II homodimeric NADP-IDHs, these two kinds of IDHs branched separately in the type II subtree. This separate branching may be due to the fact that TmIDH-like IDHs can form homotetramers in solution under some conditions<sup><a id=ref-link-section-45 title="Steen, I. H. et al. Comparison of isocitrate dehydrogenase from three hyperthermophiles reveals differences in thermostability, cofactor specificity, oligomeric state, and phylogenetic affiliation. J. Biol. Chem. 276, 43924–43931, 10.1074/jbc.M105999200 (2001)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref25>25</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-46 title="Karlstrom, M. et al. The crystal structure of a hyperthermostable subfamily II isocitrate dehydrogenase from Thermotoga maritima. FEBS J. 273, 2851–2868, 10.1111/j.1742-4658.2006.05298.x (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref30>30</a></sup>. Because all reported type II IDHs have been NADP<sup>+</sup> specific, no NAD-IDHs have been found in this subfamily. In this study, we identified the type II NAD-IDH group for the first time. This small group of distinctive IDHs was represented by IDH from <i>O</i>. <i>lucimarinus</i> CCE9901 (OlIDH), along with several counterparts that are derived from marine algae, such as <i>Micromonas</i> sp. RCC299 (MiIDH, GenBank accession number: XP_002502450). OlIDH demonstrated substantial sequence identity (&gt;30%) with typical type II homodimeric NADP-IDHs and low identity with type I IDHs (&lt;15%). Therefore, OlIDH clustered into a unique clade among the type II subfamily.</p><p>Although OlIDH was recognized as a member of the type II subfamily, its coenzyme specificities seemed to be different from those of the other type II NADP-IDHs. The crystal structure of human cytosolic homodimeric NADP-IDH, which is the most investigated type II IDH, shows that Arg353 and His354 were involved directly in coenzyme discrimination<sup><a id=ref-link-section-47 title="Xu, X. et al. Structures of human cytosolic NADP-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase reveal a novel self-regulatory mechanism of activity. J. Biol. Chem. 279, 33946–33957, 10.1074/jbc.M404298200 (2004)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref31>31</a></sup>. The conservation of these two NADP<sup>+</sup>-binding residues was confirmed by the structures of type II NADP-IDHs from <i>M. tuberculosis</i><sup><a id=ref-link-section-48 title="Quartararo, C. E., Hazra, S., Hadi, T. &amp; Blanchard, J. S. Structural, kinetic and chemical mechanism of isocitrate dehydrogenase-1 from Mycobacterium tuberculosis. Biochemistry 52, 1765–1775, 10.1021/bi400037w (2013)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref9>9</a></sup>, <i>Desulfotalea psychrophila</i><sup><a id=ref-link-section-49 title="Leiros, H. K., Fedoy, A. E., Leiros, I. &amp; Steen, I. H. The complex structures of isocitrate dehydrogenase from Clostridium thermocellum and Desulfotalea psychrophila suggest a new active site locking mechanism. FEBS Open Bio 2, 159–172, 10.1016/j.fob.2012.06.003 (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref32>32</a></sup>, <i>Thermotoga maritima</i><sup><a id=ref-link-section-50 title="Karlstrom, M. et al. The crystal structure of a hyperthermostable subfamily II isocitrate dehydrogenase from Thermotoga maritima. FEBS J. 273, 2851–2868, 10.1111/j.1742-4658.2006.05298.x (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref30>30</a></sup>, porcine mitochondria<sup><a id=ref-link-section-51 title="Ceccarelli, C., Grodsky, N. B., Ariyaratne, N., Colman, R. F. &amp; Bahnson, B. J. Crystal structure of porcine mitochondrial NADP+-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase complexed with Mn2+ and isocitrate. Insights into the enzyme mechanism. J. Biol. Chem. 277, 43454–43462, 10.1074/jbc.M207306200 (2002)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref33>33</a></sup>, and yeast mitochondria<sup><a id=ref-link-section-52 title="Peng, Y., Zhong, C., Huang, W. &amp; Ding, J. Structural studies of Saccharomyces cerevesiae mitochondrial NADP-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase in different enzymatic states reveal substantial conformational changes during the catalytic reaction. Protein Sci. 17, 1542–1554, 10.1110/ps.035675.108 (2008)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref34>34</a></sup>. However, the corresponding amino acid residues in OlIDH were substituted with Asp326 and Met327 (<a href=#f2>Fig. 2</a>). The replacement of two positively charged amino acids with one negatively charged amino acid (Asp) and one neutral amino acid with a large side chain (Met) suggests that OlIDH should favor its binding to NAD<sup>+</sup> over NADP<sup>+</sup>, because Asp in the coenzyme binding site will repel the 2'-phosphate of NADP<sup>+</sup> but can properly contact with NAD<sup>+</sup> <sup><a id=ref-link-section-53 title="Chen, R., Greer, A. &amp; Dean, A. M. A highly active decarboxylating dehydrogenase with rationally inverted coenzyme specificity. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 92, 11666–11670 (1995)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref35>35</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-54 title="Imada, K. et al. Structure and quantum chemical analysis of NAD+-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase: hydride transfer and co-factor specificity. Proteins 70, 63–71, 10.1002/prot.21486 (2008)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref36>36</a></sup>. Other suspected type II NAD-IDHs that were grouped with OlIDH also had Asp and Met (or Leu) in the corresponding sites (see <a href=/articles/srep09150#s1>Supplementary Fig. S1</a> online), suggesting that they share the same coenzyme-binding mechanism.</p><div class="border-gray-medium border-all-5 standard-space-below pl10 pr10 pt20 pb20 clear" data-container-section=figure><figure id=Fig2><figcaption><b id=f2 class="block tiny-space-below">Figure 2: Secondary structure based protein sequence alignments.</b></figcaption><div class=small-space-below><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/2 class="block small-space-below" data-track=click data-track-source=image data-track-dest=link:Figure2><img src=https://images.nature.com/m685/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-f2.jpg alt="Figure 2" class="block max-width"></a><div class="suppress-bottom-margin add-top-margin text14 sans-serif"><p>(a, b), The comparisonof OlIDH with its homologous IDH from <i>Micromonas pusilla</i>(MpIDH, GenBank Accession No. XP_003056989.1) and two typical type II homodimeric NADP-IDHs from human cytosol (HcIDH, GenBank Accession No. NP_001269316.1) and porcine mitochondria (PmIDH, GenBank Accession No. P33198.1). (c, d), The comparisonof CaIDH with its homologous IDH from <i>Campylobacter curvus</i>(CcIDH, GenBank Accession No. WP_018136314.1) and two typical monomeric NADP-IDHs from <i>Azotobacter vinelandii</i> (AvIDH, GenBank Accession No. BAA11169.1) and <i>Corynebacterium glutamicum</i> (CgIDH, GenBank Accession No. WP_011013800.1). High-resolution structure of HcIDH (PDB ID, 1T09) and AvIDH (PDB ID, 1J1W) were downloaded from the PDB database. The homology models of OlIDH and CaIDH were generated by SWISS-MODEL server (<a href=http://swissmodel.expasy.org/>http://swissmodel.expasy.org/</a>). Invariant residues are highlighted by shaded blue boxes and conserved residues by open blue boxes. The conserved residues involved in the substrate binding are indicated by empty circle (<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m3.jpg alt="" class=inline>). The core cofactor binding sites, which are the targets of the site directed mutagenesis, are indicated by red dots (<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m4.jpg alt="" class=inline>) while other cofactor binding pocket residues are indicated by black dots (<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m5.jpg alt="" class=inline>). The figure was made with ESPript 2.2.</p></div></div><div class="text-right hide-print"><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/2 class=" ml10 sans-serif pill-button inline-block" data-track=click data-track-source=button data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image"><span>Full size image</span></a></div></figure></div><p>All monomeric IDHs were separated into a monophyletic group in the phylogenetic tree (<a href=#f1>Fig. 1</a>). Two subgroups could be distinguished in the clade clearly, one of which was represented by the well-studied monomeric NADP-IDHs from <i>Azotobacter vinelandii</i>(AvIDH)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-55 title="Yasutake, Y. et al. Crystal structure of the monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase in the presence of NADP+: insight into the cofactor recognition, catalysis, and evolution. J. Biol. Chem. 278, 36897–36904, 10.1074/jbc.M304091200 (2003)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref21>21</a></sup> and <i>Corynebacterium glutamicum</i> (CgIDH)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-56 title="Imabayashi, F., Aich, S., Prasad, L. &amp; Delbaere, L. T. Substrate-free structure of a monomeric NADP isocitrate dehydrogenase: an open conformation phylogenetic relationship of isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proteins 63, 100–112, 10.1002/prot.20867 (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref20>20</a></sup>. The other, however, was newly discovered and was proposed to be NAD<sup>+</sup> specific. The representative member of this special subgroup was the <i>Campylobacter</i> sp. FOBRC14 IDH (CaIDH). CaIDH was 731 amino acids in length, which is typical for monomeric IDH. However, CaIDH shared less than 50% sequence identity with monomeric NADP-IDHs, whereas monomeric NADP-IDHs shared more than 70% sequence identity among themselves. Sequence alignment results show that the key coenzyme-binding residues, His589 and Arg600, in AvIDH<sup><a id=ref-link-section-57 title="Imabayashi, F., Aich, S., Prasad, L. &amp; Delbaere, L. T. Substrate-free structure of a monomeric NADP isocitrate dehydrogenase: an open conformation phylogenetic relationship of isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proteins 63, 100–112, 10.1002/prot.20867 (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref20>20</a></sup>, which are absolutely conserved in all monomeric NADP-IDHs, had been replaced by Leu and Asp at the corresponding sites of CaIDH (<a href=#f2>Fig. 2</a>). The presence of negatively charged Asp and neutral Leu with a large side chain eliminates the possibility of NADP<sup>+</sup> use by CaIDH, thus making CaIDH the first putative NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific monomeric IDH. Besides CaIDH, some other homogeneous monomeric NAD-IDHs, which are derived from <i>Campylobacter</i> species, such as <i>Campylobacter curvus</i> (CcIDH, GenBank accession number: WP_018136314), were also found (see <a href=/articles/srep09150#s1>Supplementary Fig. S2</a> online). These characteristic IDHs shared more than 70% sequence identity with each other, and they all had Leu and Asp (or Leu) at the putative coenzyme-binding sites (see <a href=/articles/srep09150#s1>Supplementary Fig. S2</a> online).</p><h2 class="h3 strong mb4">Overexpression, Purification, and Oligomeric State Determination</h2><p>The recombinant OlIDH and CaIDH that were tagged with 6×His were successfully produced in <i>E. coli</i> and then purified to homogeneity (<a href=#f3>Fig. 3a</a>). Purified OlIDH and CaIDH gave a single band around 45 kDa and 80 kDa in SDS-PAGE, respectively, which compared well with the theoretical molecular mass of 6×His-tagged OlIDH (46 kDa) and CaIDH (81 kDa). Size exclusion chromatography (SEC) was then performed to estimate the oligomerization status of OlIDH and CaIDH in solution. A single-elution peak was observed for OlIDH, and its native molecular mass was estimated to be 76 kDa (<a href=#f3>Fig. 3b</a>). This data can be interpreted as the protein being presented as a homodimer in solution, since the calculated mass of a monomer is 46 kDa. As for CaIDH, a dominant peak, corresponding to a molecular weight of 81 kDa, was eluted (<a href=#f3>Fig. 3c</a>). This proved that the overwhelming majority of CaIDH presented as a monomer in solution. The fraction (&lt;5%) that eluted before the main peak was calculated to be 150 kDa, and this represented a dimeric CaIDH. The dimeric CaIDH was more likely to be induced by factors, such as protein concentration, salt, temperature, and pH.</p><div class="border-gray-medium border-all-5 standard-space-below pl10 pr10 pt20 pb20 clear" data-container-section=figure><figure id=Fig3><figcaption><b id=f3 class="block tiny-space-below">Figure 3: Overexpression, purification and oligomeric state determination of the recombinant CaIDH and OlIDH.</b></figcaption><div class=small-space-below><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/3 class="block small-space-below" data-track=click data-track-source=image data-track-dest=link:Figure3><img src=https://images.nature.com/m685/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-f3.jpg alt="Figure 3" class="block max-width"></a><div class="suppress-bottom-margin add-top-margin text14 sans-serif"><p>(a), Protein purity was detected by 12% SDS-PAGE. M, protein marker; lane 1, crude extracts of cells harboring pET-28b(+) with IPTG induction; lane 2, crude extracts of cells harboring recombinant plasmid pET-<i>CaIDH</i> with IPTG induction; lane 3, purified recombinant CaIDH; lane 4, crude extracts of cells harboring recombinant plasmid pET-<i>OlIDH</i> with IPTG induction; lane 5, purified recombinant OlIDH. (b) and (c), SEC analysis of the recombinant OlIDH and CaIDH. The flow rate was 0.5 ml.min<sup>−1</sup> and the proteins in the fractions were monitored at 280 nm. <i>V</i><sub>e</sub> of the recombinant OlIDH and CaIDH were 14.23 ml and 14.21 ml, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="text-right hide-print"><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/3 class=" ml10 sans-serif pill-button inline-block" data-track=click data-track-source=button data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image"><span>Full size image</span></a></div></figure></div><p>However, molecular weight estimation by SEC is limited by the premise that the protein interacts with the column resin in an ideal way (e.g., no electrostatic or hydrophobic interactions)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-58 title="Folta-Stogniew, E. &amp; Williams, K. R. Determination of molecular masses of proteins in solution: Implementation of an HPLC size exclusion chromatography and laser light scattering service in a core laboratory. Journal of biomolecular techniques : JBT 10, 51–63 (1999)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref37>37</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-59 title="Arakawa, T., Ejima, D., Li, T. &amp; Philo, J. S. The critical role of mobile phase composition in size exclusion chromatography of protein pharmaceuticals. J. Pharm. Sci. 99, 1674–1692, 10.1002/jps.21974 (2010)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref38>38</a></sup>. In our experiment, the disagreement between the SEC estimation (76 kDa) and the deduced molecular weight of the dimeric OlIDH (92 kDa) may be due to non-ideal interactions between the protein and the SEC media. In order to accurately assign the molecular masses of the recombinant OlIDH and CaIDH, we performed the MALDI-TOF/TOF mass spectrometry. The molecular weight of the recombinant OlIDH and CaIDH were precisely determined to be 93 kDa and 82 kDa, respectively (<a href=#f4>Fig. 4</a>), demonstrating the homodimeric structure of OlIDH and the monomeric structure of CaIDH.</p><div class="border-gray-medium border-all-5 standard-space-below pl10 pr10 pt20 pb20 clear" data-container-section=figure><figure id=Fig4><figcaption><b id=f4 class="block tiny-space-below">Figure 4: MALDI-TOF/TOF MS spectra of the recombinant OlIDH (a) and CaIDH (b).</b></figcaption><div class=small-space-below><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/4 class="block small-space-below" data-track=click data-track-source=image data-track-dest=link:Figure4><img src=https://images.nature.com/m685/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-f4.jpg alt="Figure 4" class="block max-width"></a><div class="suppress-bottom-margin add-top-margin text14 sans-serif"></div></div><div class="text-right hide-print"><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/4 class=" ml10 sans-serif pill-button inline-block" data-track=click data-track-source=button data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image"><span>Full size image</span></a></div></figure></div><h2 class="h3 strong mb4">Enzyme Activity and Kinetic Characterization</h2><p>The specific activity of purified recombinant OlIDH was 72.3 U/mg with NAD<sup>+</sup> and only 3.8 U/mg with NADP<sup>+</sup>. Recombinant CaIDH showed similarly high specific activity with NAD<sup>+</sup> (53.2 U/mg) and very low activity with NADP<sup>+</sup> (8.7 U/mg). This observation primarily confirmed the NAD<sup>+</sup> preference of OlIDH and CaIDH, as suggested by the bioinformatic analysis. Kinetic characterization results show that the <i>K</i><sub>m</sub> of OlIDH for NADP<sup>+</sup> was over 16-fold greater than the <i>K</i><sub>m</sub> for NAD<sup>+</sup>. The coenzyme specificity (<i>k</i><sub>cat</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>m</sub>) of OlIDH was 99-fold greater for NAD<sup>+</sup> than NADP<sup>+</sup> (<a href=#t1>Table 1</a>). Consequently, OlIDH showed a high preference for NAD<sup>+</sup>, thus becoming the first NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific IDH in the type II subfamily. As expected, CaIDH was also characterized as an NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific IDH by kinetic analysis, and its coenzyme specificity was 61-fold greater towards NAD<sup>+</sup> than NADP<sup>+</sup> (<a href=#t1>Table 1</a>). Hence, CaIDH represents the first known monomeric NAD-IDH.</p><div class="border-gray-medium border-all-5 standard-space-below pl10 pr10 pt20 pb20 clear" data-container-section=table><figure id=Table1><figcaption><b id=t1 class="block small-space-below icon icon-table tighten-line-height">Table 1: The kinetic parameters of the wild-type and mutant enzymes</b></figcaption><div class="text-right hide-print"><a href=/articles/srep09150/tables/1 class=" ml10 sans-serif pill-button inline-block" data-track=click data-track-source=button data-track-dest="link:Table1 Full size table"><span>Full size table</span></a></div></figure></div><p>Because the newly defined type II NAD-IDHs and monomeric NAD-IDHs stand for important IDH subfamilies, identification of one enzyme for each is not sufficient enough to support their distinctiveness. We therefore characterized another two NAD-IDHs that belong to these two novel subfamilies, respectively. MiIDH, an OlIDH analog from <i>Micromonas</i> sp. RCC299, and CcIDH, a CaIDH analog from <i>Campylobacter curvus,</i> were also produced in <i>E. coli</i> and purified to homogeneity. Kinetic analysis showed that both MiIDH and CcIDH are NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific as expected, because their preference for NAD<sup>+</sup> was 224-fold and 37-fold over NADP<sup>+</sup>, respectively (<a href=#t1>Table 1</a>). The conformation of the other two NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific members from type II IDH and monomeric IDH subfamilies further validated the novelty of our finding.</p><h2 class="h3 strong mb4">Coenzyme Binding Site</h2><p>To evaluate the significance of the putative coenzyme-determining sites (Asp326 and Met327 in OlIDH, Leu584 and Asp595 in CaIDH), each mutant enzyme containing two point mutations, R<sup>326</sup>H<sup>327</sup> for OlIDH and H<sup>584</sup>R<sup>595</sup> for CaIDH, was constructed, based on the protein sequence alignment (<a href=#f2>Fig. 2</a>). The mutated enzymes were successfully produced in <i>E. coli</i> and purified to homogeneity. CD spectroscopy was performed to determine the secondary structure of wild-type and mutant enzymes (<a href=#f5>Fig. 5</a>). The results show that OlIDH R<sup>326</sup>H<sup>327</sup> and CaIDH H<sup>584</sup>R<sup>595</sup> mutants were very similar to the wild-type enzyme, thus indicating that mutations that occur at key activity sites do not cause significant changes in protein secondary structure.</p><div class="border-gray-medium border-all-5 standard-space-below pl10 pr10 pt20 pb20 clear" data-container-section=figure><figure id=Fig5><figcaption><b id=f5 class="block tiny-space-below">Figure 5: Circular diochroism (CD) spectra of the wild-type OlIDH, CaIDH and their mutants, OlIDH R<sup>326</sup>H<sup>327</sup> and CaIDH H<sup>584</sup>R<sup>595</sup>.</b></figcaption><div class=small-space-below><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/5 class="block small-space-below" data-track=click data-track-source=image data-track-dest=link:Figure5><img src=https://images.nature.com/m685/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-f5.jpg alt="Figure 5" class="block max-width"></a><div class="suppress-bottom-margin add-top-margin text14 sans-serif"><p>The CD was measured and the molar ellipticity was calculated as described in Methods section.</p></div></div><div class="text-right hide-print"><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/5 class=" ml10 sans-serif pill-button inline-block" data-track=click data-track-source=button data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image"><span>Full size image</span></a></div></figure></div><p>The kinetic characterization results are reported in <a href=#t1>Table 1</a>. The OlIDH R<sup>326</sup>H<sup>327</sup> mutant displayeda 22-fold higher <i>K</i><sub>m</sub> value for NAD<sup>+</sup> than that of the wild-type enzyme. Meanwhile, the mutant enzyme showed a greatly increased affinity to NADP<sup>+</sup>, as demonstrated by a 270-fold decrease in <i>K</i><sub>m</sub> value. The <i>k</i><sub>cat</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>m</sub> of OlIDH R<sup>326</sup>H<sup>327</sup> towards NADP<sup>+</sup> was 49-fold higher than that of the wild-type enzyme, whereas the <i>k</i><sub>cat</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>m</sub> for NAD<sup>+</sup> underwent a 44-fold decrease. Consequently, the overall specificity of the OlIDH R<sup>326</sup>H<sup>327</sup> mutant was 22-fold greater for NADP<sup>+</sup> than that for NAD<sup>+</sup>. Therefore, the two point mutations in OlIDH completely altered its coenzyme specificity, which demonstrates that Asp326 and Met327 were key specificity determinants for OlIDH.</p><p>The importance of Leu584 and Asp595 in the direct binding of NAD<sup>+</sup> to CaIDH was also confirmed by the mutagenesis study. The CaIDH H<sup>584</sup>R<sup>595</sup> mutant's affinity to NAD<sup>+</sup> was loosened, as evidenced by a17-fold elevation in <i>K</i><sub>m</sub>, as compared to that of the wild-type enzyme. By contrast, the mutant enzyme displayed a 45-fold decrease in <i>K</i><sub>m</sub> for NADP<sup>+</sup>. The <i>k</i><sub>cat</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>m</sub> of CaIDH H<sup>584</sup>R<sup>595</sup> towards NADP<sup>+</sup> was 92-fold higher than that of the wild-type enzyme, whereas the <i>k</i><sub>cat</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>m</sub> for NAD<sup>+</sup> underwent a 13-fold decrease. Consequently, the overall specificity of the CaIDH H<sup>584</sup>R<sup>595</sup> mutant was 19-fold greater for NADP<sup>+</sup> than that for NAD<sup>+</sup>. Thus, the monomeric NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific CaIDH was converted to an NADP<sup>+</sup>-dependent enzyme by two mutations in the coenzyme binding sites.</p><h2 class="h3 strong mb4">Biochemical Characterization</h2><p>The effects of pH on OlIDH and CaIDH activities were determined for the NAD<sup>+</sup>-linked reaction. OlIDH exhibited slightly different pH activity profiles and optimum pH using Mg<sup>2+</sup> or Mn<sup>2+</sup> as its cofactor. The optimum pH for OlIDH was pH 9.0 or pH 8.5 in the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> or Mn<sup>2+</sup>, respectively (<a href=#f6>Fig. 6a</a>). Furthermore, the optimum pH for CaIDH was pH 8.0 or pH 7.5 in the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> or Mn<sup>2+</sup>, respectively (<a href=#f6>Fig. 6d</a>). OlIDH showed similar pH-activity correlation with the homodimeric NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific <i>Z. mobilis</i> IDH (8.5 with Mg<sup>2+</sup>and 8.0 with Mn<sup>2+</sup>) and <i>Streptococcus suis</i> IDH (7.0 with Mg<sup>2+</sup>and 8.5 with Mn<sup>2+</sup>) from the type I subfamily<sup><a id=ref-link-section-60 title="Wang, P., Jin, M. &amp; Zhu, G. Biochemical and molecular characterization of NAD(+)-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase from the ethanologenic bacterium Zymomonas mobilis. FEMS Microbiol. Lett. 327, 134–141, 10.1111/j.1574-6968.2011.02467.x (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref6>6</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-61 title="Wang, P. et al. Enzymatic characterization of isocitrate dehydrogenase from an emerging zoonotic pathogen Streptococcus suis. Biochimie 93, 1470–1475, 10.1016/j.biochi.2011.04.021 (2011)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref39>39</a></sup>. CaIDH showed a slightly lower optimum pH when compared to the monomeric NADP-IDH from <i>Streptomyces lividans</i> TK54 (9.0 with Mg<sup>2+</sup>and 8.5 with Mn<sup>2+</sup>)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-62 title="Zhang, B. et al. Enzymatic characterization of a monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from Streptomyces lividans TK54. Biochimie 91, 1405–1410, 10.1016/j.biochi.2009.07.011 (2009)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref22>22</a></sup>.</p><div class="border-gray-medium border-all-5 standard-space-below pl10 pr10 pt20 pb20 clear" data-container-section=figure><figure id=Fig6><figcaption><b id=f6 class="block tiny-space-below">Figure 6: Effects of pH and temperature on the activity of the recombinant OlIDH and CaIDH.</b></figcaption><div class=small-space-below><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/6 class="block small-space-below" data-track=click data-track-source=image data-track-dest=link:Figure6><img src=https://images.nature.com/m685/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-f6.jpg alt="Figure 6" class="block max-width"></a><div class="suppress-bottom-margin add-top-margin text14 sans-serif"><p>(a) and (d), Effects of pH on the activity of OlIDH and CaIDH from pH 6.5 to 9.5 in the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> (<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m6.jpg alt="" class=inline>) and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m7.jpg alt="" class=inline>), respectively. (b) and (e), Effects of temperature on activity of OlIDH from 25°C to 50°C and CaIDH from 25°C to 55°C in the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> (<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m8.jpg alt="" class=inline>) and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m9.jpg alt="" class=inline>), respectively. (c) and (f), Heat-inactivation profiles of the recombinant OlIDH from 25°C to 50°C and CaIDH from 25°C to 55°C in the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> (<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m10.jpg alt="" class=inline>) and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (<img src=https://images.nature.com/full/nature-assets/srep/2015/150316/srep09150/images/srep09150-m11.jpg alt="" class=inline>), respectively.</p></div></div><div class="text-right hide-print"><a href=/articles/srep09150/figures/6 class=" ml10 sans-serif pill-button inline-block" data-track=click data-track-source=button data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image"><span>Full size image</span></a></div></figure></div><p>The optimum reaction temperature for OlIDH was around 40°C with either Mg<sup>2+</sup> or Mn<sup>2+</sup> as the cofactor (<a href=#f6>Fig. 6b</a>). Results from heat inactivation studies demonstrate that recombinant OlIDH was stable below 45°C, but its activity rapidly declined as the temperature was raised. Incubation at 45°C for 20 min caused a 28% or 21% loss of activity in the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> or Mn<sup>2+</sup> (<a href=#f6>Fig. 6c</a>), respectively, whereas incubation at 50°C caused a 91% or 84% loss of activity in the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> or Mn<sup>2+</sup>, respectively (<a href=#f6>Fig. 6c</a>). The optimum temperature for CaIDH activity was around 45°C or 40°C in the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> or Mn<sup>2+</sup>, respectively (<a href=#f6>Fig. 6e</a>). Recombinant CaIDH retained the majority of the activity below 45°C. However, its activity dropped rapidly as the temperature was raised. Incubation at 55°C for 20 min caused a 55% or 75% loss of activity in the presence of Mg<sup>2+</sup> or Mn<sup>2+</sup>, respectively (<a href=#f6>Fig. 6f</a>).</p><p>The effects of different metal ions on the activities of OlIDH and CaIDH were examined (<a href=#t2>Table 2</a>). Both enzymes needed the presence of a divalent cation for catalysis, although fractions of activities were observed for OlIDH (5.8%) and CaIDH (7.9%) when no metal ions were added. Mn<sup>2+</sup> was the most favorable cation for both OlIDH and CaIDH, and its role could be largely replaced by Mg<sup>2+</sup> (68.6% for OlIDH and 78.3% for CaIDH). Mn<sup>2+</sup> has also been found to be the preferred cation for other homodimeric NAD-IDHs from <i>Streptococcus mutans</i>, <i>S. suis</i> and <i>Z. mobilis</i><sup><a id=ref-link-section-63 title="Wang, P., Jin, M. &amp; Zhu, G. Biochemical and molecular characterization of NAD(+)-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase from the ethanologenic bacterium Zymomonas mobilis. FEMS Microbiol. Lett. 327, 134–141, 10.1111/j.1574-6968.2011.02467.x (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref6>6</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-64 title="Wang, P. et al. Enzymatic characterization of isocitrate dehydrogenase from an emerging zoonotic pathogen Streptococcus suis. Biochimie 93, 1470–1475, 10.1016/j.biochi.2011.04.021 (2011)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref39>39</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-65 title="Wang, A. et al. Heteroexpression and characterization of a monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from the multicellular prokaryote Streptomyces avermitilis MA-4680. Mol. Biol. Rep. 38, 3717–3724, 10.1007/s11033-010-0486-3 (2011)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref40>40</a></sup>. In addition, Mn<sup>2+</sup> has been determined as the most favored metal ion for monomeric NADP-IDHs<sup><a id=ref-link-section-66 title="Zhang, B. et al. Enzymatic characterization of a monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from Streptomyces lividans TK54. Biochimie 91, 1405–1410, 10.1016/j.biochi.2009.07.011 (2009)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref22>22</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-67 title="Wang, A. et al. Heteroexpression and characterization of a monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from the multicellular prokaryote Streptomyces avermitilis MA-4680. Mol. Biol. Rep. 38, 3717–3724, 10.1007/s11033-010-0486-3 (2011)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref40>40</a></sup>. OlIDH was completely inactivated by either 2 mM Cu<sup>2+</sup> or Zn<sup>2+</sup>, whereas Co<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> caused a 79.5% and 39% loss in activity, respectively. Other metal ions, such as Ca<sup>2+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, and K<sup>+</sup>, slightly affected OlIDH activity in the presence of Mn<sup>2+</sup>. CaIDH was eliminated by 2 mM Zn<sup>2+</sup>and severely down regulated by Co<sup>2+</sup> (49%), Ca<sup>2+</sup> (74%), and Cu<sup>2+</sup> (77%). Monovalent ions (K<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, Rb<sup>+</sup>, Li<sup>+</sup>) slightly improved CaIDH activity.</p><div class="border-gray-medium border-all-5 standard-space-below pl10 pr10 pt20 pb20 clear" data-container-section=table><figure id=Table2><figcaption><b id=t2 class="block small-space-below icon icon-table tighten-line-height">Table 2: Effects of metal ions on the activity of the recombinant OlIDH and CaIDH</b></figcaption><div class="text-right hide-print"><a href=/articles/srep09150/tables/2 class=" ml10 sans-serif pill-button inline-block" data-track=click data-track-source=button data-track-dest="link:Table2 Full size table"><span>Full size table</span></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby=discussion><div class="serif article-section js-article-section cleared clear" id=discussion-section><h2 class="js-section-title section-title position-relative strong tighten-line-height background-gray-light pt20 pb6 pl20 pr20 standard-space-below mq640-pt20 mq640-pb10 mq640-mt0 js-extend-left small-space-above" id=discussion>Discussion</h2><div class="pl20 mq875-pl0 js-collapsible-section" id=discussion-content><p>In the present study, four novel IDHs, belong to two novel IDH subfamilies, were reported for the first time. Two of them were the NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific homodimeric IDHs from marine alga, <i>O</i>. <i>lucimarinus</i> (OlIDH) and <i>Micromonas</i> sp. RCC299 (MiIDH), and the other two were the NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific monomeric IDH from pathogens <i>Campylobacter</i> sp. FOBRC14 (CaIDH) and <i>Campylobacter curvus</i> (CcIDH). OlIDH and MiIDH were found to be the first NAD-IDHs in the type II subfamily, as all members of this subfamily were previously thought to be NADP<sup>+</sup> specific. CaIDH and CuIDH, however, were found to be the first NAD<sup>+</sup> specific monomeric IDHs. These four IDHs, together with their NAD<sup>+</sup> specific counterparts, constituted two separate branches on the phylogenetic tree. Most previous studies have proposed that IDH favors NADP<sup>+</sup> over NAD<sup>+</sup> as a coenzyme<sup><a id=ref-link-section-68 title="Zhang, B. et al. Enzymatic characterization of a monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from Streptomyces lividans TK54. Biochimie 91, 1405–1410, 10.1016/j.biochi.2009.07.011 (2009)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref22>22</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-69 title="Steen, I. H. et al. Comparison of isocitrate dehydrogenase from three hyperthermophiles reveals differences in thermostability, cofactor specificity, oligomeric state, and phylogenetic affiliation. J. Biol. Chem. 276, 43924–43931, 10.1074/jbc.M105999200 (2001)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref25>25</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-70 title="Chen, R. &amp; Yang, H. A highly specific monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from Corynebacterium glutamicum. Arch Biochem Biophys 383, 238–245, 10.1006/abbi.2000.2082 (2000)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref27>27</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-71 title="Xu, X. et al. Structures of human cytosolic NADP-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase reveal a novel self-regulatory mechanism of activity. J. Biol. Chem. 279, 33946–33957, 10.1074/jbc.M404298200 (2004)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref31>31</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-72 title="Peng, Y., Zhong, C., Huang, W. &amp; Ding, J. Structural studies of Saccharomyces cerevesiae mitochondrial NADP-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase in different enzymatic states reveal substantial conformational changes during the catalytic reaction. Protein Sci. 17, 1542–1554, 10.1110/ps.035675.108 (2008)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref34>34</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-73 title="Wang, A. et al. Heteroexpression and characterization of a monomeric isocitrate dehydrogenase from the multicellular prokaryote Streptomyces avermitilis MA-4680. Mol. Biol. Rep. 38, 3717–3724, 10.1007/s11033-010-0486-3 (2011)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref40>40</a></sup>. However, as more and more NAD-IDHs from diverse backgrounds being reported, NAD<sup>+</sup> appears to be widely used by IDH through nature<sup><a id=ref-link-section-74 title="Wang, P., Jin, M. &amp; Zhu, G. Biochemical and molecular characterization of NAD(+)-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase from the ethanologenic bacterium Zymomonas mobilis. FEMS Microbiol. Lett. 327, 134–141, 10.1111/j.1574-6968.2011.02467.x (2012)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref6>6</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-75 title="Stokke, R. et al. Biochemical characterization of isocitrate dehydrogenase from Methylococcus capsulatus reveals a unique NAD+-dependent homotetrameric enzyme. Arch. Microbiol. 187, 361–370, 10.1007/s00203-006-0200-y (2007)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref26>26</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-76 title="Wang, P. et al. Enzymatic characterization of isocitrate dehydrogenase from an emerging zoonotic pathogen Streptococcus suis. Biochimie 93, 1470–1475, 10.1016/j.biochi.2011.04.021 (2011)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref39>39</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-77 title="Wang, P., Song, P., Jin, M. &amp; Zhu, G. Isocitrate dehydrogenase from Streptococcus mutans: biochemical properties and evaluation of a putative phosphorylation site at Ser102. PLoS One 8, e58918, 10.1371/journal.pone.0058918 (2013)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref41>41</a></sup>.</p><p>By adding two groups of OlIDH-like and CaIDH-like NAD-IDHs, we have expanded and refined the evolutionary classification of the IDH protein family. The phylogenetic tree presented in <a href=#f1>Fig. 1</a> contains three well-supported monophyletic groups: type I IDH, type II IDH, and monomeric IDH. Monomeric IDHs were also included in the present phylogenetic analysis for the integrity of IDH family, and the same principle was applied by Delbaere et al.<sup><a id=ref-link-section-78 title="Imabayashi, F., Aich, S., Prasad, L. &amp; Delbaere, L. T. Substrate-free structure of a monomeric NADP isocitrate dehydrogenase: an open conformation phylogenetic relationship of isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proteins 63, 100–112, 10.1002/prot.20867 (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref20>20</a></sup>. The most important advancement was the discovery of type II homodimeric NAD-IDHs and monomeric NAD-IDHs, which completed the classification of the IDH protein family in the view of coenzyme specificity. Neither of these groups of NAD-IDH have been reported in previous studies<sup><a id=ref-link-section-79 title="Zhu, G., Golding, G. B. &amp; Dean, A. M. The selective cause of an ancient adaptation. Science 307, 1279–1282, 10.1126/science.1106974 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref4>4</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-80 title="Dean, A. M. &amp; Golding, G. B. Protein engineering reveals ancient adaptive replacements in isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 94, 3104–3109 (1997)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref23>23</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-81 title="Steen, I. H., Lien, T. &amp; Birkeland, N. K. Biochemical and phylogenetic characterization of isocitrate dehydrogenase from a hyperthermophilic archaeon, Archaeoglobus fulgidus. Arch. Microbiol. 168, 412–420 (1997)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref24>24</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-82 title="Steen, I. H. et al. Comparison of isocitrate dehydrogenase from three hyperthermophiles reveals differences in thermostability, cofactor specificity, oligomeric state, and phylogenetic affiliation. J. Biol. Chem. 276, 43924–43931, 10.1074/jbc.M105999200 (2001)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref25>25</a></sup>. IDHs with NAD<sup>+</sup> specificity are ancestral to NADP-IDHs, and this evolutionary hypothesis has been demonstrated by experimental reverse evolution, which was applied to the typical type I <i>E. coli</i> NADP-IDH<sup><a id=ref-link-section-83 title="Zhu, G., Golding, G. B. &amp; Dean, A. M. The selective cause of an ancient adaptation. Science 307, 1279–1282, 10.1126/science.1106974 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref4>4</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-84 title="Dean, A. M. &amp; Golding, G. B. Protein engineering reveals ancient adaptive replacements in isocitrate dehydrogenase. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 94, 3104–3109 (1997)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref23>23</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-85 title="Chen, R., Greer, A. &amp; Dean, A. M. A highly active decarboxylating dehydrogenase with rationally inverted coenzyme specificity. Proc Natl Acad Sci U S A 92, 11666–11670 (1995)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref35>35</a></sup>. The findings of NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific OlIDH and CaIDH, together with their homologous proteins, will help identify the possible ancestors of type II IDHs and monomeric IDHs, respectively.</p><p>By searching the genome of <i>O</i>. <i>lucimarinus</i> CCE9901 (GenBank Assembly ID: GCA_000092065.1), one copy of <i>IDH</i> gene could be found, which suggests that OlIDH is likely the only functional IDH isozyme in this marine algae. The genus <i>Ostreococcus</i> is composed of a group of globally distributed, photosynthetic, unicellular green algae, and these cells are the smallest known eukaryotes<sup><a id=ref-link-section-86 title="Lanier, W., Moustafa, A., Bhattacharya, D. &amp; Comeron, J. M. EST analysis of Ostreococcus lucimarinus, the most compact eukaryotic genome, shows an excess of introns in highly expressed genes. PLoS One 3, e2171, 10.1371/journal.pone.0002171 (2008)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref42>42</a></sup>. Because all known NAD-IDHs from eukaryotes are hetero-oligomeric and consist of at least two different subunits<sup><a id=ref-link-section-87 title="Taylor, A. B., Hu, G., Hart, P. J. &amp; McAlister-Henn, L. Allosteric motions in structures of yeast NAD+-specific isocitrate dehydrogenase. J. Biol. Chem. 283, 10872–10880, 10.1074/jbc.M708719200 (2008)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref3>3</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-88 title="Hu, G., Lin, A. P. &amp; McAlister-Henn, L. Physiological consequences of loss of allosteric activation of yeast NAD+-specific isocitrate dehydrogenase. J. Biol. Chem. 281, 16935–16942, 10.1074/jbc.M512281200 (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref43>43</a></sup>, OlIDH represents the first eukaryotic homodimeric NAD-IDH. Furthermore, IDHs from some other marine algae, such as <i>Micromonas</i> sp. RCC299, <i>Emiliania huxleyi</i> and <i>Thalassiosira oceanica</i>(see <a href=/articles/srep09150#s1>Supplementary Table S1</a> online), showed high homology to OlIDH (&gt;70%) and branched together with OlIDH on the phylogenetic tree. Considering the antiquity of oceanic algae, the ancient trait of NAD<sup>+</sup> specificity that was possessed by IDHs in these organisms can be fairly explained.</p><p>Similar to OlIDH, the monomeric CaIDH is likely the only active IDH isozyme found in <i>Campylobacter</i> sp. FOBRC14 (GenBank Assembly ID: GCA_000287855.1). The monomeric NAD-IDH group seemed to be very small in composition, because only IDHs from the genus <i>Campylobacter</i> were included (see <a href=/articles/srep09150#s1>Supplementary Table S1</a> online). These monomeric IDHs shared very high sequence identity (&gt;60%), and the putative NAD<sup>+</sup>-binding sites were conservative (<a href=#f2>Fig. 2</a>). <i>Campylobacter</i> is the most common cause of bacterial foodborne illness and has drawn a lot of attention in recent years<sup><a id=ref-link-section-89 title="Deshpande, N. P. et al. Sequencing and validation of the genome of a Campylobacter concisus reveals intra-species diversity. PLoS One 6, e22170, 10.1371/journal.pone.0022170 (2011)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref44>44</a>,<a id=ref-link-section-90 title="Deshpande, N. P., Kaakoush, N. O., Wilkins, M. R. &amp; Mitchell, H. M. Comparative genomics of Campylobacter concisus isolates reveals genetic diversity and provides insights into disease association. BMC Genomics 14, 585, 10.1186/1471-2164-14-585 (2013)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref45>45</a></sup>. As a pathogen, it is surprising that the glyoxylate bypass is absent in <i>Campylobacter</i> sp. FOBRC14, as no genes encoding isocitrate lyase and malate synthase can be found in its genome. The glyoxylate bypass ensures the bypass of two oxidative steps of the TCA cycle and permits the net incorporation of carbon during the growth of most microorganisms on acetate or fatty acids as the primary carbon source. The end products of the bypass can be used for gluconeogenesis and other biosynthetic processes<sup><a id=ref-link-section-91 title="Dunn, M. F., Ramirez-Trujillo, J. A. &amp; Hernandez-Lucas, I. Major roles of isocitrate lyase and malate synthase in bacterial and fungal pathogenesis. Microbiology 155, 3166–3175, 10.1099/mic.0.030858-0 (2009)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref46>46</a></sup>. Most intracellular human pathogens, such as <i>Salmonella typhimurium</i><sup><a id=ref-link-section-92 title="Tchawa Yimga, M. et al. Role of gluconeogenesis and the tricarboxylic acid cycle in the virulence of Salmonella enterica serovar Typhimurium in BALB/c mice. Infect. Immun. 74, 1130–1140, 10.1128/IAI.74.2.1130-1140.2006 (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref47>47</a></sup>, <i>Burkholderia pseudomallei</i><sup><a id=ref-link-section-93 title="van Schaik, E. J., Tom, M. &amp; Woods, D. E. Burkholderia pseudomallei isocitrate lyase is a persistence factor in pulmonary melioidosis: implications for the development of isocitrate lyase inhibitors as novel antimicrobials. Infect. Immun. 77, 4275–4283, 10.1128/IAI.00609-09 (2009)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref48>48</a></sup> and <i>M. tuberculosis</i><sup><a id=ref-link-section-94 title="Munoz-Elias, E. J. &amp; McKinney, J. D. Mycobacterium tuberculosis isocitrate lyases 1 and 2 are jointly required for in vivo growth and virulence. Nat. Med. 11, 638–644, 10.1038/nm1252 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref49>49</a></sup>, need the glyoxylate bypass for their virulence, because fatty acids are the only abundant sources of C2 carbon in mammalian tissues<sup><a id=ref-link-section-95 title="McKinney, J. D. et al. Persistence of Mycobacterium tuberculosis in macrophages and mice requires the glyoxylate shunt enzyme isocitrate lyase. Nature 406, 735–738, 10.1038/35021074 (2000)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref50>50</a></sup>. Interestingly, the existence of the glyoxylate bypass in microorganisms has always been accompanied by at least one NADP-IDH isozyme, which provides the majority of NADPH to support bacterial growth on limited carbon sources<sup><a id=ref-link-section-96 title="Zhu, G., Golding, G. B. &amp; Dean, A. M. The selective cause of an ancient adaptation. Science 307, 1279–1282, 10.1126/science.1106974 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref4>4</a></sup>. Because the glyoxylate bypass could not be detectedin <i>Campylobacter</i> sp. FOBRC14, it is understandable that an NAD<sup>+</sup>-specific IDH, rather than an NADP<sup>+</sup>-specific IDH, was found in this organism. The finding also suggests that the infection mechanism of <i>Campylobacter</i> sp. FOBRC14 may be different from that of pathogens with the glyoxylate bypass.</p><p>As an eukaryotic NAD-IDH, OlIDH shared very similar kinetic properties with hetero-oligomeric NAD-IDHs from other eukaryotic cells. The <i>K</i><sub>m</sub> value for NAD<sup>+</sup> of OlIDH (136.6 μM) was in a similar range of those determined for <i>Yarrowia lipolytica</i> yeast (136 μM)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-97 title="Morgunov, I. G., Solodovnikova, N. Y., Sharyshev, A. A., Kamzolova, S. V. &amp; Finogenova, T. V. Regulation of NAD(+)-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase in the citrate producing yeast Yarrowia lipolytica. Biochemistry (Mosc) 69, 1391–1398 (2004)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref51>51</a></sup> and rats (148.9 μM)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-98 title="Qi, F., Chen, X. &amp; Beard, D. A. Detailed kinetics and regulation of mammalian NAD-linked isocitrate dehydrogenase. Biochim. Biophys. Acta 1784, 1641–1651, 10.1016/j.bbapap.2008.07.001 (2008)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref52>52</a></sup>. It was higher than that for humans (70 μM)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-99 title="Soundar, S., O'Hagan, M., Fomulu, K. S. &amp; Colman, R. F. Identification of Mn2+-binding aspartates from alpha, beta, and gamma subunits of human NAD-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase. J. Biol. Chem. 281, 21073–21081, 10.1074/jbc.M602956200 (2006)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref53>53</a></sup> and lower than that for budding yeast (210 μM)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-100 title="Dean, A. M. A molecular investigation of genotype by environment interactions. Genetics 139, 19–33 (1995)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref54>54</a></sup>. When compared to homodimeric NADP-IDHs of type II subfamily, OlIDH showed much lower affinity to its coenzyme than NADP-IDHs do to NADP<sup>+</sup>, such as in the wild pig (5.6 μM)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-101 title="Huang, Y. C. &amp; Colman, R. F. Location of the coenzyme binding site in the porcine mitochondrial NADP-dependent isocitrate dehydrogenase. J. Biol. Chem. 280, 30349–30353, 10.1074/jbc.M505828200 (2005)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref55>55</a></sup>, rat (11.5 μM)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-102 title="Jennings, G. T., Minard, K. I. &amp; McAlister-Henn, L. Expression and mutagenesis of mammalian cytosolic NADP+-specific isocitrate dehydrogenase. Biochemistry 36, 13743–13747, 10.1021/bi970916r (1997)." href=/articles/srep09150#ref56>56</a></sup> and budding yeast (20 μM)<sup><a id=ref-link-section-103 title="Lu, Q., Minard, K. I. &amp; McAlister-Henn, L. Dual compartmental localization and function of mammalian NADP+-specific isocitrate dehydrogenase in yeast.
